					<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span lang="en" dir="ltr"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Diffusion of innovations</span></span></h1>
							
<div id="p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown mw-portlet mw-portlet-lang"  >
	<input type="checkbox" id="p-lang-btn-checkbox" role="button" aria-haspopup="true" data-event-name="ui.dropdown-p-lang-btn" class="vector-dropdown-checkbox mw-interlanguage-selector" aria-label="Go to an article in another language. Available in 29 languages"   >

<span class="vector-dropdown-label-text">29 languages</span>
	</label>
	<div class="vector-dropdown-content">

		<div class="vector-menu-content">

</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Theory on how and why new ideas spread</div>

Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Diffusion+of+innovations%22">"Diffusion of innovations"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Diffusion+of+innovations%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Diffusion+of+innovations%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Diffusion+of+innovations%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Diffusion+of+innovations%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Diffusion+of+innovations%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2023</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Diffusion_of_ideas.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Diffusion_of_ideas.svg/330px-Diffusion_of_ideas.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="248" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Diffusion_of_ideas.svg/960px-Diffusion_of_ideas.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a><figcaption>The diffusion of innovations according to Rogers. With successive groups of consumers adopting the new technology (shown in blue), its <a href="/wiki/Market_share" title="Market share">market share</a> (yellow) will eventually reach the saturation level. The blue curve is broken into sections of adopters.</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Diffusion of innovations</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Theory" title="Theory">theory</a> that seeks to explain how, why, and at what rate new <a href="/wiki/Idea" title="Idea">ideas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> spread. The theory was popularized by <a href="/wiki/Everett_Rogers" title="Everett Rogers">Everett Rogers</a> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Diffusion_of_Innovations" title="Diffusion of Innovations">Diffusion of Innovations</a></i>, first published in 1962.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Rogers5&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rogers5-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rogers argues that diffusion is the process by which an <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">innovation</a> is communicated through certain channels over time among the participants in a social system. The origins of the diffusion of innovations theory are varied and span multiple disciplines. This concept has also influenced modern design and human-computer interaction.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2026)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Rogers proposes that five main elements influence the spread of a new idea: the innovation itself, adopters, <a href="/wiki/Communication_channel" title="Communication channel">communication channels</a>, time, and a social system. This process relies heavily on <a href="/wiki/Social_capital" title="Social capital">social capital</a>. The innovation must be widely adopted in order to self-sustain. Within the rate of adoption, there is a point at which an innovation reaches <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass_(sociodynamics)" title="Critical mass (sociodynamics)">critical mass</a>. In 1989, management consultants working at the consulting firm <a href="/wiki/Regis_McKenna#Regis_McKenna,_Inc." title="Regis McKenna">Regis McKenna, Inc.</a> theorized that this point lies at the boundary between the early adopters and the early majority. This gap between niche appeal and mass (self-sustained) adoption was originally labeled "the marketing chasm".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The categories of adopters are innovators, <a href="/wiki/Early_adopters" class="mw-redirect" title="Early adopters">early adopters</a>, early majority, late majority, and laggards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1962150&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1962150-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Diffusion manifests itself in different ways and is highly subject to the type of adopters and innovation-decision process. The criterion for the adopter categorization is innovativeness, defined as the degree to which an individual adopts a new idea.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Trans-cultural_diffusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Trans-cultural diffusion">concept of diffusion</a> was first studied by the French <a href="/wiki/Sociologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Sociologist">sociologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Tarde" title="Gabriel Tarde">Gabriel Tarde</a> in late 19th century<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FFID&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FFID-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and by German and Austrian <a href="/wiki/Anthropologists" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropologists">anthropologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geographers" class="mw-redirect" title="Geographers">geographers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Ratzel" title="Friedrich Ratzel">Friedrich Ratzel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leo_Frobenius" title="Leo Frobenius">Leo Frobenius</a>. The study of diffusion of innovations took off in the subfield of <a href="/wiki/Rural_sociology" title="Rural sociology">rural sociology</a> in the midwestern United States in the 1920s and 1930s. Agriculture technology was advancing rapidly, and researchers started to examine how independent farmers were adopting hybrid seeds, equipment, and techniques.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A study of the adoption of hybrid corn seed in Iowa by Ryan and Gross (1943) solidified the prior work on diffusion into a distinct paradigm that would be cited consistently in the future.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-chla.library.cornell.edu&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chla.library.cornell.edu-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since its start in rural sociology, Diffusion of Innovations has been applied to numerous contexts, including <a href="/wiki/Medical_sociology" title="Medical sociology">medical sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Development_studies" title="Development studies">development studies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health_promotion" title="Health promotion">health promotion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organizational_behavior" title="Organizational behavior">organizational studies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_management" title="Knowledge management">knowledge management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation biology</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Complex_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Complex systems">complexity studies</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Greenhalgh&#95;2005&#95;417–430&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenhalgh_2005_417–430-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with a particularly large impact on the use of medicines, medical techniques, and health communications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In organizational studies, its basic epidemiological or internal-influence form was formulated by H. Earl Pemberton,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> such as postage stamps and standardized school ethics codes.
</p><p>In 1962, <a href="/wiki/Everett_Rogers" title="Everett Rogers">Everett Rogers</a>, a professor of rural sociology at <a href="/wiki/Ohio_State_University" title="Ohio State University">Ohio State University</a>, published his seminal work: <i>Diffusion of Innovations</i>. Rogers synthesized research from over 508 diffusion studies across the fields that initially influenced the theory: <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, early sociology, <a href="/wiki/Rural_sociology" title="Rural sociology">rural sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_sociology" title="Industrial sociology">industrial sociology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medical_sociology" title="Medical sociology">medical sociology</a>. Rogers applied it to the healthcare setting to address issues with hygiene, cancer prevention, family planning, and drunk driving. Using his synthesis, Rogers produced a theory of the adoption of innovations among individuals and organizations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers196283&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers196283-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Diffusion of Innovations</i> and Rogers' later books are among the most often cited in diffusion research. His methodologies are closely followed in recent diffusion research, even as the field has expanded into, and been influenced by, other methodological disciplines such as <a href="/wiki/Social_network_analysis" title="Social network analysis">social network analysis</a> and communication.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RogersShoemaker1971&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RogersShoemaker1971-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Elements">Elements</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Elements"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The key elements in diffusion research are:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Element
</th>
<th>Definition
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Innovation
</td>
<td>Innovation is a broad category, relative to the current knowledge of the analyzed unit. Any idea, practice, or object that is perceived as new by an individual or other unit of adoption could be considered an innovation available for study.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers198311&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers198311-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Adopters
</td>
<td>Adopters are the minimal unit of analysis. In most studies, adopters are individuals, but can also be organizations (businesses, schools, hospitals, etc.), clusters within social networks, or countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Communication channels
</td>
<td>Diffusion, by definition, takes place among people or organizations. Communication channels allow the transfer of information from one unit to the other.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers198317&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers198317-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Communication patterns or capabilities must be established between parties as a minimum for diffusion to occur.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Time
</td>
<td>The passage of time is necessary for innovations to be adopted; they are rarely adopted instantaneously. In fact, in the Ryan and Gross (1943) study on hybrid corn adoption, adoption occurred over more than ten years, and most farmers only dedicated a fraction on their fields to the new corn in the first years after adoption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chla.library.cornell.edu&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chla.library.cornell.edu-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers198321,&#95;23&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers198321,_23-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Social system
</td>
<td>The social system is the combination of external influences (mass media, surfactants, organizational or governmental mandates) and internal influences (<a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_ties" title="Interpersonal ties">strong and weak social relationships</a>, distance from <a href="/wiki/Opinion_leadership" title="Opinion leadership">opinion leaders</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are many roles in a social system, and their combination represents the total influences on a potential adopter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers198324&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers198324-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Characteristics_of_innovations">Characteristics of innovations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Characteristics of innovations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Studies have explored many characteristics of innovations. Meta-reviews have identified several characteristics that are common among most studies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These are in line with the characteristics that Rogers initially cited in his reviews.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1962&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1962-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Rogers describes five characteristics that potential adopters evaluate when deciding whether to adopt an innovation (his 'ACCORD' model):<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1962&#95;23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1962-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Advantage: In what way is this innovation better than the alternatives?</li>
<li>Compatibility: How well does this innovation fit with existing values, patterns of behavior, or tools?</li>
<li>Complexity / Simplicity: The easier it is to learn or grasp, the faster it diffuses.</li>
<li>Observability: Are its benefits noticeable? If someone else is using the innovation, can I see it being used?</li>
<li>Divisibility / Trialability: Can you try it before you buy it?</li></ol>
<p>These qualities interact and are judged as a whole. For example, an innovation might be extremely complex, reducing its likelihood to be adopted and diffused, but it might be very compatible with a large advantage relative to current tools. Even with this high learning curve, potential adopters might adopt the innovation anyway.
</p><p>Studies also identify other characteristics of innovations, but these are not as common as the ones that Rogers lists above.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The fuzziness of the boundaries of the innovation can impact its adoption. Specifically, innovations with a small core and large periphery are easier to adopt.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Innovations that are less risky are easier to adopt as the potential loss from failed integration is lower.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Meyer&#95;1988&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer_1988-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Innovations that are disruptive to routine tasks, even when they bring a large relative advantage, might not be adopted because of added instability. Likewise, innovations that make tasks easier are likely to be adopted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Closely related to relative complexity, knowledge requirements are the ability barrier to use presented by the difficulty to use the innovation. Even when there are high knowledge requirements, support from prior adopters or other sources can increase the chances for adoption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Characteristics_of_individual_adopters">Characteristics of individual adopters</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Characteristics of individual adopters"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Like innovations, adopters have been determined to have traits that affect their likelihood to adopt an innovation. A bevy of individual personality traits have been explored for their impacts on adoption, but with little agreement.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ability and motivation, which vary on situation unlike personality traits, have a large impact on a potential adopter's likelihood to adopt an innovation. Unsurprisingly, potential adopters who are motivated to adopt an innovation are likely to make the adjustments needed to adopt it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Motivation can be impacted by the meaning that an innovation holds; innovations can have symbolic value that encourage (or discourage) adoption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> First proposed by Ryan and Gross (1943), the overall connectedness of a potential adopter to the broad community represented by a city.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chla.library.cornell.edu&#95;6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chla.library.cornell.edu-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Potential adopters who frequent metropolitan areas are more likely to adopt an innovation. Finally, potential adopters who have the power or agency to create change, particularly in organizations, are more likely to adopt an innovation than someone with less power over his choices.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Complementary to the diffusion framework, behavioral models such as <a href="/wiki/Technology_acceptance_model" title="Technology acceptance model">Technology acceptance model</a> (TAM) and <a href="/wiki/Unified_theory_of_acceptance_and_use_of_technology" title="Unified theory of acceptance and use of technology">Unified theory of acceptance and use of technology</a> (UTAUT) are frequently used to understand individual technology adoption decisions in greater details.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Characteristics_of_organizations">Characteristics of organizations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Characteristics of organizations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Organizations face more complex adoption possibilities because organizations are both the aggregate of its individuals and its own system with a set of procedures and norms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Three organizational characteristics match well with the individual characteristics above: tension for change (motivation and ability), innovation-system fit (compatibility), and assessment of implications (observability). Organizations can feel pressured by a tension for change. If the organization's situation is untenable, it will be motivated to adopt an innovation to change its fortunes. This tension often plays out among its individual members. Innovations that match the organization's pre-existing system require fewer coincidental changes and are easy to assess and more likely to be adopted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The wider environment of the organization, often an industry, community, or economy, exerts pressures on the organization, too. Where an innovation is diffusing through the organization's environment for any reason, the organization is more likely to adopt it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Meyer&#95;1988&#95;26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meyer_1988-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Innovations that are intentionally spread, including by political mandate or directive, are also likely to diffuse quickly.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>h individual decisions where behavioral models (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Technology_acceptance_model" title="Technology acceptance model">TAM</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unified_theory_of_acceptance_and_use_of_technology" title="Unified theory of acceptance and use of technology">UTAUT</a>) can be used to complement the diffusion framework and reveal further details, these models are not directly applicable to organizational decisions. However, research suggested that simple behavioral models can still be used as a good predictor of organizational technology adoption when proper initial screening procedures are introduced.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Li2020&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li2020-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Process">Process</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Process"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Transtheoretical_model#Stages_of_change" title="Transtheoretical model">Transtheoretical model §&#160;Stages of change</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:DoI_Stages.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/DoI_Stages.jpg/250px-DoI_Stages.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="163" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/DoI_Stages.jpg 2x" data-file-width="424" data-file-height="277" /></a><figcaption></figcaption></figure>
<p>Diffusion occurs through a five–step decision-making process. It occurs through a series of communication channels over a period of time among the members of a similar social system. Ryan and Gross first identified adoption as a process in 1943.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers196279&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers196279-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rogers' five stages (steps): awareness, interest, evaluation, trial, and adoption are integral to this theory. An individual might reject an innovation at any time during or after the adoption process. Abrahamson examined this process critically by posing questions such as: How do technically inefficient innovations diffuse and what impedes technically efficient innovations from catching on? Abrahamson makes suggestions for how organizational scientists can more comprehensively evaluate the spread of innovations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In later editions of <i>Diffusion of Innovation</i>, Rogers changes his terminology of the five stages to: knowledge, persuasion, decision, implementation, and confirmation. However, the descriptions of the categories have remained similar throughout the editions.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Five stages of the adoption process
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Stage
</th>
<th>Definition
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Knowledge / Awareness
</td>
<td>The individual is first exposed to an innovation, but lacks information about the innovation. During this stage the individual has not yet been inspired to find out more information about the innovation.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Persuasion
</td>
<td>The individual is interested in the innovation and actively seeks related information/details.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Decision
</td>
<td>The individual takes the concept of the change and weighs the advantages/disadvantages of using the innovation and decides whether to adopt or reject the innovation. Due to the individualistic nature of this stage, Rogers notes that it is the most difficult stage on which to acquire empirical evidence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers196283&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers196283-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Implementation
</td>
<td>The individual employs the innovation to a varying degree depending on the situation. During this stage the individual also determines the usefulness of the innovation and may search for further information about it.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Confirmation / Continuation
</td>
<td>The individual finalizes their decision to continue using the innovation. This stage is both intrapersonal (may cause <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_dissonance" title="Cognitive dissonance">cognitive dissonance</a>) and interpersonal, confirmation the group has made the right decision. This stage allows the adopter to seek reassurance that the decision and implementation are beneficial. Adopters typically experience cognitive dissonance without this final confirmation. Dissonance could be heightened by negative information about the innovation, and if dissonance is not relieved, the innovation may be discounted to restore balance. Change agents help adopters in this stage feel comfortable with their decision.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Decisions">Decisions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Decisions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Two factors determine what type a particular decision is:
</p>
<ul><li>Whether the decision is made freely and implemented voluntarily</li>
<li>Who makes the decision</li></ul>
<p>Based on these considerations, three types of innovation-decisions have been identified.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Type
</th>
<th>Definition
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Optional Innovation-Decision
</td>
<td>made by an individual who is in some way distinguished from others.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Collective Innovation-Decision
</td>
<td>made collectively by all participants.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Authority Innovation-Decision
</td>
<td>made for the entire social system by individuals in positions of influence or power.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Rate_of_adoption">Rate of adoption</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Rate of adoption"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The rate of adoption is defined as the relative speed at which participants adopt an innovation. Rate is usually measured by the length of time required for a certain percentage of the members of a social system to adopt an innovation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1962134&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1962134-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The rates of adoption for innovations are determined by an individual's adopter category. In general, individuals who first adopt an innovation require a shorter adoption period (adoption process) when compared to late adopters.
</p><p>Within the <a href="/wiki/Adoption_curve" class="mw-redirect" title="Adoption curve">adoption curve</a> at some point the innovation reaches <a href="/wiki/Critical_mass_(sociodynamics)" title="Critical mass (sociodynamics)">critical mass</a>. This is when the number of individual adopters ensures that the innovation is self-sustaining.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Adoption_strategies">Adoption strategies</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Adoption strategies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Rogers outlines several strategies in order to help an innovation reach this stage, including when an innovation adopted by a highly respected individual within a <a href="/wiki/Social_network" title="Social network">social network</a> and creating an instinctive desire for a specific innovation. Another strategy includes injecting an innovation into a group of individuals who would readily use said technology, as well as providing positive reactions and benefits for early adopters.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Diffusion_vis-à-vis_adoption"><span id="Diffusion_vis-.C3.A0-vis_adoption"></span>Diffusion vis-à-vis adoption</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Diffusion vis-à-vis adoption"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Adoption is an individual process detailing the series of stages one undergoes from first hearing about a product to finally adopting it. Diffusion signifies a group phenomenon, which suggests how an innovation spreads.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Adopter_categories">Adopter categories</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Adopter categories"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Rogers defines an adopter category as a classification of individuals within a <a href="/wiki/Social_system" title="Social system">social system</a> on the basis of innovativeness. In the book <i>Diffusion of Innovations</i>, Rogers suggests a total of five categories of adopters in order to standardize the usage of adopter categories in diffusion research. The adoption of an innovation follows an <a href="/wiki/Sigmoid_function" title="Sigmoid function">S curve</a> when plotted over a length of time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The categories of adopters are innovators, <a href="/wiki/Early_adopters" class="mw-redirect" title="Early adopters">early adopters</a>, early majority, late majority and laggards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1962150&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1962150-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition to the gatekeepers and opinion leaders who exist within a given community, change agents may come from outside the community. Change agents bring innovations to new communities – first through the gatekeepers, then through the opinion leaders, and so on through the community.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Adopter category
</th>
<th>Definition
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Innovators
</td>
<td>Innovators are willing to take risks, have the highest social status, have financial liquidity, are social and have closest contact to scientific sources and interaction with other innovators. Their risk tolerance allows them to adopt technologies that may ultimately fail. Financial resources help absorb these failures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1962282&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1962282-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Early_adopters" class="mw-redirect" title="Early adopters">Early adopters</a>
</td>
<td>These individuals have the highest degree of <a href="/wiki/Opinion_leadership" title="Opinion leadership">opinion leadership</a> among the adopter categories. Early adopters have a higher social status, financial liquidity, advanced education and are more socially forward than late adopters. They are more discreet in adoption choices than innovators. They use judicious choice of adoption to help them maintain a central communication position.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1962283&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1962283-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Early Majority
</td>
<td>They adopt an innovation after a varying degree of time that is significantly longer than the innovators and early adopters. Early Majority have above average social status, contact with early adopters and seldom hold positions of <a href="/wiki/Opinion_leadership" title="Opinion leadership">opinion leadership</a> in a system (<a href="#CITEREFRogers1962">Rogers 1962</a>, p.&#160;283)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Late Majority
</td>
<td>They adopt an innovation after the average participant. These individuals approach an innovation with a high degree of skepticism and after the majority of society has adopted the innovation. Late Majority are typically skeptical about an innovation, have below average social status, little financial liquidity, in contact with others in late majority and early majority and little <a href="/wiki/Opinion_leadership" title="Opinion leadership">opinion leadership</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Laggards
</td>
<td>They are the last to adopt an innovation. Unlike some of the previous categories, individuals in this category show little to no opinion leadership. These individuals typically have an aversion to change-agents. Laggards typically tend to be focused on "traditions", lowest social status, lowest financial liquidity, oldest among adopters, and in contact with only family and close friends.
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Failed_diffusion">Failed diffusion</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Failed diffusion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Failed diffusion does not mean that the technology was adopted by no one. Rather, failed diffusion often refers to diffusion that does not reach or approach 100% adoption due to its own weaknesses, competition from other innovations, or simply a lack of awareness. From a social networks perspective, a failed diffusion might be widely adopted within certain clusters but fail to make an impact on more distantly related people. Networks that are over-connected might suffer from a rigidity that prevents the changes an innovation might bring, as well.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
Sometimes, some innovations also fail as a result of lack of local involvement and community participation.
</p><p>For example, Rogers discussed a situation in Peru involving the implementation of boiling drinking water to improve health and wellness levels in the village of Los Molinos. The residents had no knowledge of the link between sanitation and illness. The campaign worked with the villagers to try to teach them to boil water, burn their garbage, install latrines and report cases of illness to local health agencies. In Los Molinos, a stigma was linked to boiled water as something that only the "unwell" consumed, and thus, the idea of healthy residents boiling water prior to consumption was frowned upon. The two-year educational campaign was considered to be largely unsuccessful. This failure exemplified the importance of the roles of the communication channels that are involved in such a campaign for social change. An examination of diffusion in <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> determined that there can be more than one social network at play as innovations are communicated. One network carries information and the other carries influence. While people might hear of an innovation's uses, in Rogers' Los Molinos sanitation case, a network of influence and status prevented adoption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Heterophily_and_communication_channels">Heterophily and communication channels</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Heterophily and communication channels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Lazarsfeld and Merton first called attention to the principles of <a href="/wiki/Homophily" title="Homophily">homophily</a> and its opposite, <a href="/wiki/Heterophily" title="Heterophily">heterophily</a>. Using their definition, Rogers defines homophily as "the degree to which pairs of individuals who interact are similar in certain attributes, such as beliefs, education, social status, and the like".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers198318&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers198318-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When given the choice, individuals usually choose to interact with someone similar to themselves. Homophilous individuals engage in more effective communication because their similarities lead to greater knowledge gain as well as attitude or behavior change. As a result, homophilous people tend to promote diffusion among each other.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, diffusion requires a certain degree of heterophily to introduce new ideas into a relationship; if two individuals are identical, no diffusion occurs because there is no new information to exchange. Therefore, an ideal situation would involve potential adopters who are homophilous in every way, except in knowledge of the innovation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers198319&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers198319-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>In the field of innovation studies, network analysis has been increasingly applied to investigate how patterns of collaboration and connectivity influence the diffusion of new ideas and technologies. Recent contributions include *Elements of Network Science: Theory, Methods and Applications in Stata, R and Python* by Antonio Zinilli (2025), which integrates theoretical and applied perspectives on network science with a focus on socio-economic systems and innovation processes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zinilli2025&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zinilli2025-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Empirical studies by Zinilli and co-authors have examined the structural dynamics of inter-city innovation networks in China using temporal exponential random graph models (TERGM), highlighting the role of network evolution in shaping innovation flows,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as the configuration of innovation networks among European city-regions, investigating whether they function as exclusive clubs or inclusive hubs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Promotion of healthy behavior provides an example of the balance required of homophily and heterophily. People tend to be close to others of similar health status.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a result, people with unhealthy behaviors like smoking and obesity are less likely to encounter information and behaviors that encourage good health. This presents a critical challenge for health communications, as ties between heterophilous people are relatively weaker, harder to create, and harder to maintain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Developing heterophilous ties to unhealthy communities can increase the effectiveness of the diffusion of good health behaviors. Once one previously homophilous tie adopts the behavior or innovation, the other members of that group are more likely to adopt it, too.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Role_of_social_systems">Role of social systems</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Role of social systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_the_Roman_Empire_as_diffusion_of_innovation" title="Christianization of the Roman Empire as diffusion of innovation">Christianization of the Roman Empire as diffusion of innovation</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Opinion_leaders">Opinion leaders</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Opinion leaders"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Not all individuals exert an equal amount of influence over others. In this sense <a href="/wiki/Opinion_leadership" title="Opinion leadership">opinion leaders</a> are influential in spreading either positive or negative information about an innovation. Rogers relies on the ideas of Katz &amp; Lazarsfeld and the <a href="/wiki/Two-step_flow" class="mw-redirect" title="Two-step flow">two-step flow</a> theory in developing his ideas on the influence of opinion leaders.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-KatzLazarsfeld1970&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KatzLazarsfeld1970-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Opinion leaders have the most influence during the evaluation stage of the innovation-decision process and on late adopters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1962219&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1962219-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition opinion leaders typically have greater exposure to the mass media, more cosmopolitan, greater contact with change agents, more social experience and exposure, higher socioeconomic status, and are more innovative than others.
</p><p>Research was done in the early 1950s at the University of Chicago attempting to assess the cost-effectiveness of broadcast advertising on the diffusion of new products and services.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The findings were that opinion leadership tended to be organized into a hierarchy within a society, with each level in the hierarchy having most influence over other members in the same level, and on those in the next level below it. The lowest levels were generally larger in numbers and tended to coincide with various demographic attributes that might be targeted by mass advertising. However, it found that direct word of mouth and example were far more influential than broadcast messages, which were only effective if they reinforced the direct influences. This led to the conclusion that advertising was best targeted, if possible, on those next in line to adopt, and not on those not yet reached by the chain of influence.
</p><p>Research on <a href="/wiki/Actor%E2%80%93network_theory" title="Actor–network theory">actor-network theory (ANT)</a> also identifies a significant overlap between the ANT concepts and the diffusion of innovation which examine the characteristics of innovation and its context among various interested parties within a social system to assemble a network or system which implements innovation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other research relating the concept to <a href="/wiki/Public_choice_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Public choice theory">public choice theory</a> finds that the hierarchy of influence for innovations need not, and likely does not, coincide with hierarchies of official, political, or economic status.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Elites are often not innovators, and innovations may have to be introduced by outsiders and propagated up a hierarchy to the top decision makers.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Electronic_communication_social_networks">Electronic communication social networks</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Electronic communication social networks"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Prior to the introduction of the Internet, it was argued that social networks had a crucial role in the diffusion of innovation particularly <a href="/wiki/Tacit_knowledge" title="Tacit knowledge">tacit knowledge</a> in the book <i>The IRG Solution – hierarchical incompetence and how to overcome it</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Andrews1984&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews1984-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The book argued that the widespread adoption of computer networks of individuals would lead to much better diffusion of innovations, with greater understanding of their possible shortcomings and the identification of needed innovations that would not have otherwise occurred. The social model proposed by Ryan and Gross<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers196279&#95;38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers196279-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is expanded by Valente who uses social networks as a basis for adopter categorization instead of solely relying on the system-level analysis used by Ryan and Gross. Valente also looks at an individual's personal network, which is a different application than the organizational perspective espoused by many other scholars.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Valente,&#95;T.W.&#95;1996&#95;Pages&#95;69&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valente,_T.W._1996_Pages_69-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Recent research by Wear shows, that particularly in regional and rural areas, significantly more innovation takes place in communities which have stronger inter-personal networks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Organizations">Organizations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Organizations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Innovations are often adopted by organizations through two types of innovation-decisions: collective innovation decisions and authority innovation decisions. The collective decision occurs when adoption is by consensus. The authority decision occurs by adoption among very few individuals with high positions of power within an organization.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers2003403&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers2003403-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Unlike the optional innovation decision process, these decision processes only occur within an organization or hierarchical group. Research indicated that, with proper initial screening procedures, even simple behavioral model can serve as a good predictor for technology adoption in many commercial organizations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Li2020&#95;37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li2020-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Within an organization certain individuals are termed "champions" who stand behind an innovation and break through opposition. The champion plays a very similar role as the champion used within the efficiency business model <a href="/wiki/Six_Sigma" title="Six Sigma">Six Sigma</a>. The process contains five stages that are slightly similar to the innovation-decision process that individuals undertake. These stages are: <a href="/wiki/Agenda-setting" class="mw-redirect" title="Agenda-setting">agenda-setting</a>, matching, redefining/restructuring, clarifying and routinizing.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Networks_and_environment">Networks and environment</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Networks and environment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A firm's interaction with other players, along with its environment and <a href="/wiki/Organizational_culture" title="Organizational culture">organizational culture</a>, are key in the social theory of diffusion.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_use_of_networks">The use of networks</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: The use of networks"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The effects of networks and institutional environment on adoption of innovations can be explained using a <a href="/wiki/Social_network_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Social network theory">social network theory</a> model. In such a model, nodes represent agents (e.g. companies or organizations) and ties represent a connection between two entities (e.g. a company-client relationship or <a href="/wiki/Competition_(economics)" title="Competition (economics)">competitive relationship</a>). Diffusion occurs when a novel idea, product, or process is implemented by an agent and permeates through these ties to others.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Strang&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Internal_and_external_diffusion">Internal and external diffusion</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Internal and external diffusion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Diffusion of information and ideas has been categorized into two modes:
</p><p><b>Internal diffusion</b> is the spread of information and innovations within a network, flowing within a single adopting population&#160;&#8211;&#32; a given industry or geographical network. Internal diffusion dynamics require that innovative and <a href="/wiki/Early_adopter" title="Early adopter">early adopter</a> firms introduce new ideas into a network, which are then picked up by the majority of firms and laggard firms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1983&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1983-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2026)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> DiMaggio and Powell (1983)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DiMaggio&#95;and&#95;Powell&#95;1983&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiMaggio_and_Powell_1983-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  argue that firms search for the best ideas and practices and mimic new ideas that prove to work. This phenomenon is known as <a href="/wiki/Mimetic_isomorphism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mimetic isomorphism">mimetic isomorphism</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DiMaggio&#95;and&#95;Powell&#95;1983&#95;69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiMaggio_and_Powell_1983-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and ironically may lead to clustering of firm structure and practices.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Strang&#95;67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, firms are often forced to adopt new ideas as they are constantly competing with other firms; that is, firms want to seem modernized and seek legitimacy in implementing innovative practices.
</p><p><b>External diffusion</b> refers to the introduction of ideas to a network from outside actors: firms or other agents on the edge of the network. Outside actors include the mass media and "change agents." <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">Mass media</a> can amplify trends and movements that occur in the marketplace, introducing new innovations to network members, exposing "<a href="/wiki/Best_practice" title="Best practice">best-practice</a>" ideas, and conveying new principles.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers1983&#95;68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers1983-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2026)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Change agents are usually business professionals (such as lawyers, consultants, bankers, or politicians) who spread new practices or aid in promoting new ideas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Strang&#95;67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  These individuals often introduce business models, legal strategies, or investment techniques that are picked up by several entities within a network and continue to diffuse. Often, such external diffusion leads to conformity of a set of corporate strategies or structures, a phenomenon DiMaggio and Powell called "normative isomorphism".
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Environmental_and_cultural_factors_of_diffusion">Environmental and cultural factors of diffusion</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Environmental and cultural factors of diffusion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An agent's environmental and cultural makeup influence the decision to adopt an idea diffusing through a network. Some of the major characteristics of firms that influence their decision to innovate are <i>clustering</i>, <i>weak ties</i>, and firm size.
</p><p><i>Clustering</i>, the existence of a group of tightly connected agents, is a frequent concept in network theory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It includes, for example, similar firms locating themselves in close proximity to each other (Silicon Valley for technology firms; New York for banking services). Such clustering and close proximity increases the diffusion rate of ideas for firms within a cluster, as other firms are more likely to adopt an idea if another firm has adopted it within its cluster.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Strang&#95;67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An agent with <i>weak ties</i> has a connection to two or more clusters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These agents are integral in connecting groups, as they provide communication between large clusters. Firms with weak ties can be isolated firms, firms with business in two or more spaces, or those which are external change agents. Firms with weak ties introduce clusters to new, proven methods.
</p><p>Firm size has been shown to have an influence on the rate of diffusion. Strang and Soule (1998) have shown that large, technical, and specialized organizations with informal cultures tend to innovate much faster than other firms. Smaller and more rigid firms attempt to mimic these "early adopters" in attempt to keep up with competition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Strang&#95;67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Strang-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Extensions_of_the_theory">Extensions of the theory</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Extensions of the theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Policy">Policy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Policy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Diffusion of Innovations has been applied beyond its original domains.
In the case of political science and administration, policy diffusion focuses on how institutional innovations are adopted by other institutions, at the local, state, or country level. An alternative term is 'policy transfer' where the focus is more on the agents of diffusion and the diffusion of policy knowledge, such as in the work of <a href="/wiki/Diane_Stone" title="Diane Stone">Diane Stone</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Specifically, policy transfer can be defined as "knowledge about how policies administrative arrangements, institutions, and ideas in one political setting (past or present) is used in the development of policies, administrative arrangements, institutions, and ideas in another political setting".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The first interests with regards to policy diffusion were focused in time variation or state lottery adoption,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but more recently interest has shifted towards mechanisms (emulation, learning and coercion)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or in channels of diffusion<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where researchers find that <a href="/wiki/Regulatory_agency" title="Regulatory agency">regulatory agency</a> creation is transmitted by country and sector channels. At the local level, examining popular city-level policies make it easy to find patterns in diffusion through measuring public awareness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At the international level, economic policies have been thought to transfer among countries according to local politicians' learning of successes and failures elsewhere and outside mandates made by global financial organizations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a group of countries succeed with a set of policies, others follow, as exemplified by the deregulation and liberalization across the developing world after the successes of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Asian_Tigers" title="Four Asian Tigers">Asian Tigers</a>. The reintroduction of regulations in the early 2000s also shows this learning process, which would fit under the stages of knowledge and decision, can be seen as lessons learned by following China's successful growth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Technology">Technology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Technology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Peres, Muller and Mahajan suggested that diffusion is "the process of the market penetration of new products and services that is driven by social influences, which include all interdependencies among consumers that affect various market players with or without their explicit knowledge".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Eveland evaluated diffusion from a phenomenological view, stating, "Technology is information, and exists only to the degree that people can put it into practice and use it to achieve values".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Diffusion of existing technologies has been measured using "S curves". These technologies include radio, television, VCR, cable, flush toilet, clothes washer, refrigerator, home ownership, air conditioning, dishwasher, electrified households, telephone, cordless phone, cellular phone, per capita airline miles, personal computer and the Internet. These data<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> can act as a predictor for future innovations.
</p><p>Diffusion curves for <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> reveal contrasts in the diffusion process of personal technologies versus infrastructure.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Theme">Theme</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Theme"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The scientific literature identifies in the works of Rogers (1976)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Mahajan &amp; Muller (1994)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> an intrinsic aspect of the theory of diffusion of innovations: the focus on the use of data on products, ideas/patents, and technologies. As the development of this research paradigm was paved mainly by studies on the diffusion of products and/or services (Im, Mason &amp; Houston, 2007;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Lassar, Manolis &amp; Lassar, 2005<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), little was structured to consider a theme as a basis for studies on diffusion, as in the work of Takahashi, Figueiredo &amp; Favaretto (2023)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who analyzed the diffusion of Deep Learning in <a href="/wiki/BRICS" title="BRICS">BRICS</a> and <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries using data from <a href="/wiki/Google_Trends" title="Google Trends">Google Trends</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Consequences_of_adoption">Consequences of adoption</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Consequences of adoption"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Both positive and negative outcomes are possible when an individual or organization chooses to adopt a particular innovation. Rogers states that this area needs further research because of the biased positive attitude that is associated with innovation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERogers2003470&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERogers2003470-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rogers lists three categories for consequences: desirable vs. undesirable, direct vs. indirect, and anticipated vs. unanticipated.
</p><p>In contrast Wejnert details two categories: public vs. private and benefits vs. costs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWejnert2002299&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWejnert2002299-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Public_vis-à-vis_private"><span id="Public_vis-.C3.A0-vis_private"></span>Public vis-à-vis private</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Public vis-à-vis private"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Public consequences comprise the impact of an innovation on those other than the actor, while private consequences refer to the impact on the actor. Public consequences usually involve collective actors, such as countries, states, organizations or social movements. The results are usually concerned with issues of societal well-being. Private consequences usually involve individuals or small collective entities, such as a community. The innovations are usually concerned with the improvement of quality of life or the reform of organizational or social structures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWejnert2002301&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWejnert2002301-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Benefits_vis-à-vis_costs"><span id="Benefits_vis-.C3.A0-vis_costs"></span>Benefits vis-à-vis costs</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Benefits vis-à-vis costs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Benefits of an innovation obviously are the positive consequences, while the costs are the negative. Costs may be monetary or nonmonetary, direct or indirect. Direct costs are usually related to financial uncertainty and the economic state of the actor. Indirect costs are more difficult to identify. An example would be the need to buy a new kind of pesticide to use innovative seeds. Indirect costs may also be social, such as social conflict caused by innovation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWejnert2002301&#95;92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWejnert2002301-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Marketers are particularly interested in the diffusion process as it determines the success or failure of a new product. It is quite important for a marketer to understand the diffusion process so as to ensure proper management of the spread of a new product or service.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Intended_vis-à-vis_unintended"><span id="Intended_vis-.C3.A0-vis_unintended"></span>Intended vis-à-vis unintended</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Intended vis-à-vis unintended"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The diffusion of innovations theory has been used to conduct research on the unintended consequences of new interventions in public health. In the book multiple examples of the unintended negative consequences of technological diffusion are given. The adoption of automatic tomato pickers developed by Midwest agricultural colleges led to the adoption of harder tomatoes (disliked by consumers) and the loss of thousands of jobs leading to the collapse of thousands of small farmers. In another example, the adoption of snowmobiles in Saami reindeer herding culture is found to lead to the collapse of their society with widespread alcoholism and unemployment for the herders, ill-health for the reindeer (such as stress ulcers, miscarriages) and a huge increase in inequality.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Mathematical_treatment">Mathematical treatment</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diffusion_of_innovations&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Mathematical treatment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Logistic_function" title="Logistic function">Logistic function</a></div>
<p>The diffusion of an innovation typically follows an S-shaped curve which often resembles a <a href="/wiki/Logistic_function" title="Logistic function">logistic function</a>. Roger's diffusion model concludes that the popularity of a new product will grow with time to a saturation level and then decline, but it cannot predict how much time it will take and what the saturation level will be. Bass (1969)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and many other researchers proposed modeling the diffusion based on parametric formulas to fill this gap and to provide a means for a quantitative forecast of adoption timing and levels. The <a href="/wiki/Bass_diffusion_model" title="Bass diffusion model">Bass model</a> focuses on the first two (Introduction and Growth).
